src="/w/load.php?debug=false&amp;lang=en&amp;modules=html5shiv&amp;only=scripts&amp;skin=vector&amp;sync=1"></script><![endif]-->\n</head>\n<body class="mediawiki ltr sitedir-ltr mw-hide-empty-elt ns-0 ns-subject page-International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_1814\xe2\x80\x931919 rootpage-International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_1814\xe2\x80\x931919 skin-vector action-view">\t\t<div id="mw-page-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="mw-head-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">International relations of the Great Powers (1814\xe2\x80\x931919)</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Big_four.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Big_four.jpg/280px-Big_four.jpg" width="280" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Big_four.jpg/420px-Big_four.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Big_four.jpg/560px-Big_four.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2616" data-file-height="2008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Big_four.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Big_Four_(World_War_I)" title="The Big Four (World War I)">The "Big Four"</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference of 1919</a>: <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vittorio_Emanuele_Orlando" title="Vittorio Emanuele Orlando">Vittorio Emanuele Orlando</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Clemenceau" title="Georges Clemenceau">Georges Clemenceau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a></div></div></div>\n<p>This article covers worldwide diplomacy and, more generally, the <b>international relations</b> of the <a href="/wiki/Major_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Major power">major powers</a> from 1814 to 1919. The international relations of minor countries are covered in their own history articles. This era covers the period from the end of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> (1814\xe2\x80\x9315), to the end of the <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_history_of_World_War_I" title="Diplomatic history of World War I">First World War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a>. For the previous era see <a href="/wiki/International_relations,_1648%E2%80%931814" title="International relations, 1648\xe2\x80\x931814">International relations, 1648\xe2\x80\x931814</a>. For the 1920s and 1930s see <a href="/wiki/International_relations_(1919%E2%80%931939)" title="International relations (1919\xe2\x80\x931939)">International relations (1919\xe2\x80\x931939)</a>.\n</p><p>Important themes include the rapid industrialization and growing power of Britain, France and Germany, and, later in the period, the United States and Japan. This led to <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialist</a> competitions for influence and power throughout the world, most famously the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a> in the 1880s and 1890s. The reverberations are still widespread and consequential in the 21st-century. Britain established an informal economic network that, combined with its colonies and its Royal Navy, made it the hegemonic nation until its power was challenged by Germany. It was a peaceful century, with no wars between the great powers, apart from the 1854\xe2\x80\x931871 interval, and some small wars between Russia and the Ottoman Empire. After 1900 there were a series of wars in the Balkan region, which exploded out of control into  <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_history_of_World_War_I" title="Diplomatic history of World War I">World War I</a> (1914\xe2\x80\x9318)\xe2\x80\x94a massively devastating event that was unexpected in its timing, duration, casualties, and long-term impact.\n</p><p>In 1814 diplomats recognised five Great Powers: France, Britain, Russia, Austria (in 1867\xe2\x80\x931918, Austria-Hungary) and <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> (in 1871 the German Empire). Italy was added to this group after <a href="/wiki/Risorgimento" class="mw-redirect" title="Risorgimento">its unification</a> and on the eve of the First World War there were two major blocs in Europe: the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Entente" title="Triple Entente">Triple Entente</a> formed by <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Alliance_(1882)" title="Triple Alliance (1882)">Triple Alliance</a> formed by <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Greece" title="Kingdom of Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(Spain)" title="Restoration (Spain)">Spain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> were smaller powers. <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Romania" title="Kingdom of Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bulgaria" title="Kingdom of Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Serbia" title="Kingdom of Serbia">Serbia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Montenegro" title="Kingdom of Montenegro">Montenegro</a> initially operated as autonomous vassals for they were legally still part of the declining <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, which may also be included among the major powers, before gaining their independence.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> By 1905 two rapidly growing non-European states, <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, had joined the Great Powers. The Great War unexpectedly tested their military, diplomatic, social and economic capabilities to the limit.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Germany, Austria-Hungary and the Ottoman Empire were defeated and lost their great power status, as well as great deal of territory. The winners Britain, France, Italy and Japan gained permanent seats at the governing council of the new <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>. The United States, meant to be the fifth permanent member, decided to operate independently and never joined the League. For the following periods see <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_history_of_World_War_I" title="Diplomatic history of World War I">Diplomatic history of World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_relations_(1919%E2%80%931939)" title="International relations (1919\xe2\x80\x931939)">International relations (1919\xe2\x80\x931939)</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toctext">Korea</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-45"><a href="#Dividing_up_China"><span class="tocnumber">6.8</span> <span class="toctext">Dividing up China</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-46"><a href="#British_policies_3"><span class="tocnumber">6.9</span> <span class="toctext">British policies</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-47"><a href="#Free_trade_imperialism"><span class="tocnumber">6.9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Free trade imperialism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-48"><a href="#Splendid_isolation"><span class="tocnumber">6.9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Splendid isolation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-49"><a href="#Policy_toward_Germany"><span class="tocnumber">6.9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Policy toward Germany</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-50"><a href="#Liberal_Party_splits_on_imperialism"><span class="tocnumber">6.9.4</span> <span class="toctext">Liberal Party splits on imperialism</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-51"><a href="#The_Eastern_Question"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">The Eastern Question</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-52"><a href="#Long-term_goals"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Long-term goals</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-53"><a href="#Ottoman_Empire_(Turkey)"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Ottoman Empire (Turkey)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-54"><a href="#Austro-Hungarian_Empire"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Austro-Hungarian Empire</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-55"><a href="#Russia"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Russia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-56"><a href="#Serbia"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Serbia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-57"><a href="#Germany_2"><span class="tocnumber">7.1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Germany</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-58"><a href="#Great_Eastern_Crisis_of_1875-78_Turkey_at_war_with_Serbia_and_Russia"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Great Eastern Crisis of 1875-78 Turkey at war with Serbia and Russia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-59"><a href="#Minority_rights"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Minority rights</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-60"><a href="#British_policies_4"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">British policies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-61"><a href="#German_policy,_1872\xe2\x80\x931890"><span class="tocnumber">7.5</span> <span class="toctext">German policy, 1872\xe2\x80\x931890</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-62"><a href="#War_in_Sight_crisis_of_1875"><span class="tocnumber">7.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">War in Sight crisis of 1875</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-63"><a href="#The_alliance_between_Russia_and_France,_1894\xe2\x80\x931914"><span class="tocnumber">7.6</span> <span class="toctext">The alliance between Russia and France, 1894\xe2\x80\x931914</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-64"><a href="#Balkan_crises:_1908-1913"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Balkan crises: 1908-1913</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-65"><a href="#Bosnian_crisis_of_1908\xe2\x80\x9309"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Bosnian crisis of 1908\xe2\x80\x9309</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-66"><a href="#Balkan_Wars"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Balkan Wars</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-67"><a href="#Coming_of_World_War"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Coming of World War</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-68"><a href="#France_2"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">France</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-69"><a href="#Franco-Russian_Alliance"><span class="tocnumber">9.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Franco-Russian Alliance</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-70"><a href="#Anglo-German_relations_deteriorate:_1880-1904"><span class="tocnumber">9.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Anglo-German relations deteriorate: 1880-1904</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-71"><a href="#Two_crises_in_Morocco"><span class="tocnumber">9.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Two crises in Morocco</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-72"><a href="#British-German_naval_race"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">British-German naval race</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-73"><a href="#The_Great_War"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">The Great War</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-74"><a href="#Paris_Peace_Conference_and_Versailles_Treaty_1919"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Paris Peace Conference and Versailles Treaty 1919</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-75"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-76"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-77"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-78"><a href="#Surveys"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Surveys</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-79"><a href="#Maps"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">Maps</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-80"><a href="#Coming_of_World_War_I"><span class="tocnumber">14.3</span> <span class="toctext">Coming of World War I</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-81"><a href="#Primary_sources_on_coming_of_the_war"><span class="tocnumber">14.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Primary sources on coming of the war</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-82"><a href="#Wartime_diplomacy"><span class="tocnumber">14.4</span> <span class="toctext">Wartime diplomacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-83"><a href="#Imperialism_2"><span class="tocnumber">14.5</span> <span class="toctext">Imperialism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-84"><a href="#Britain"><span class="tocnumber">14.6</span> <span class="toctext">Britain</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-85"><a href="#Primary_sources_for_Britain"><span class="tocnumber">14.6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Primary sources for Britain</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-86"><a href="#France_3"><span class="tocnumber">14.7</span> <span class="toctext">France</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-87"><a href="#Germany_and_Austria"><span class="tocnumber">14.8</span> <span class="toctext">Germany and Austria</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-88"><a href="#Russia_and_Balkans"><span class="tocnumber">14.9</span> <span class="toctext">Russia and Balkans</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-89"><a href="#United_States"><span class="tocnumber">14.10</span> <span class="toctext">United States</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-90"><a href="#Japan_and_China"><span class="tocnumber">14.11</span> <span class="toctext">Japan and China</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-91"><a href="#Others"><span class="tocnumber">14.12</span> <span class="toctext">Others</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-92"><a href="#Primary_sources"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Primary sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span id="1814.E2.80.931830:_Restoration_and_reaction"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1814\xe2\x80\x931830:_Restoration_and_reaction">1814\xe2\x80\x931830: Restoration and reaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: 1814\xe2\x80\x931830: Restoration and reaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>For the previous diplomatic era, see <a href="/wiki/International_relations,_1648%E2%80%931814" title="International relations, 1648\xe2\x80\x931814">International relations, 1648\xe2\x80\x931814</a><br />\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Concert_of_Europe" title="Concert of Europe">Concert of Europe</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_1815_map_en.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Europe_1815_map_en.png/400px-Europe_1815_map_en.png" width="400" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Europe_1815_map_en.png/600px-Europe_1815_map_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Europe_1815_map_en.png/800px-Europe_1815_map_en.png 2x" data-file-width="2284" data-file-height="1503" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe_1815_map_en.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">The national boundaries within Europe as set by the Congress of Vienna, 1815.</div></div></div></div>\n<p>As the four major European powers (<a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Prussia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austria</a>) opposing the <a href="/wiki/First_French_Empire" title="First French Empire">French Empire</a> in the Napoleonic Wars saw Napoleon\'s power collapsing in 1814, they started planning for the postwar world. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Chaumont" title="Treaty of Chaumont">Treaty of Chaumont</a> of March 1814 reaffirmed decisions that had been made already and which would be ratified by the more important Congress of Vienna of 1814\xe2\x80\x9315. They included the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">confederated Germany</a> including both Austria and Prussia (plus the Czech lands), the division of French protectorates and annexations into independent states, the restoration of the Bourbon kings of Spain, the enlargement of the Netherlands to include what in 1830 became modern Belgium, and the continuation of British subsidies to its allies. The Treaty of Chaumont united the powers to defeat Napoleon and became the cornerstone of the Concert of Europe, which formed the balance of power for the next two decades.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One goal of diplomacy throughout the period was to achieve a "balance of power," so that no one or two powers would be dominant.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>  If one power gained an advantage \xe2\x80\x93 for example by winning a war and acquiring new territory-- its rivals might seek "compensation" -- that is, territorial or other gains, even though they were not part of the war in the first place.  The bystander might be angry if the winner of the war did not provide enough compensation. For example in 1866, a German coalition defeated Austria, but France was angry that it did not get any compensation to balance off the German gains.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<h3><span id="Congress_of_Vienna:_1814.E2.80.931815"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Congress_of_Vienna:_1814\xe2\x80\x931815">Congress of Vienna: 1814\xe2\x80\x931815</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Congress of Vienna: 1814\xe2\x80\x931815">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a></div>\n<p>The Congress of Vienna (1814\xe2\x80\x931815) dissolved the Napoleonic world and attempted to restore the monarchies Napoleon had overthrown, ushering in an era of reaction.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Klemens_von_Metternich" title="Klemens von Metternich">Metternich</a>, the prime minister of Austria (1809\xe2\x80\x9348), and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stewart,_Viscount_Castlereagh" title="Robert Stewart, Viscount Castlereagh">Lord Castlereagh</a>, the foreign minister of Great Britain (1812\xe2\x80\x931822), the Congress set up a system to preserve the peace. Under the <a href="/wiki/Concert_of_Europe" title="Concert of Europe">Concert of Europe</a> (or "Congress system"), the major European powers\xe2\x80\x94Britain, Russia, Prussia, Austria, and (after 1818) France\xe2\x80\x94pledged to meet regularly to resolve differences. This plan was the first of its kind in European history and seemed to promise a way to collectively manage European affairs and promote peace. It was the forerunner of the League of Nations and the United Nations but it collapsed by 1823.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Congress resolved the <a href="/wiki/Polish-Saxon_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish-Saxon crisis">Polish\xe2\x80\x93Saxon crisis</a> at Vienna and the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Laibach#Eastern_Question" title="Congress of Laibach">question of Greek independence</a> at <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Laibach" title="Congress of Laibach">Laibach</a>. <a href="/wiki/Concert_of_Europe" title="Concert of Europe">Three major European congresses</a> took place. The <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Aix-la-Chapelle_(1818)" title="Congress of Aix-la-Chapelle (1818)">Congress of Aix-la-Chapelle (1818)</a> ended the military occupation of France and adjusted downward the 700 million francs the French were obligated to pay as reparations. The Russian tsar proposed the formation of an entirely new alliance, to include all of the signatories from the Vienna treaties, to guarantee the sovereignty, territorial integrity, and preservation of the ruling governments of all members of this new coalition. The  tsar further proposed an international army, with the Russian army as its nucleus, to provide the wherewithal to intervene in any country that needed it. Lord Castlereagh saw this as a highly undesirable commitment to reactionary policies.  He recoiled at the idea of Russian armies marching across Europe to put down popular uprisings. Furthermore, to admit all the smaller countries would create intrigue and confusion. Britain refused to participate, so the idea was abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The other meetings proved meaningless as each nation realized the Congresses were not to their advantage, where disputes were resolved with a diminishing degree of effectiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-C.W._Crawley_1830._pp_669-71_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-C.W._Crawley_1830._pp_669-71-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roy_Bridge_1979_pp_34_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_Bridge_1979_pp_34-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To achieve lasting peace, the <a href="/wiki/Concert_of_Europe" title="Concert of Europe">Concert of Europe</a> tried to maintain the balance of power. Until the 1860s the territorial boundaries laid down at the Congress of Vienna were maintained, and even more important there was an acceptance of the theme of balance with no major aggression.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Otherwise, the Congress system had "failed" by 1823.<sup id="cite_ref-Roy_Bridge_1979_pp_34_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_Bridge_1979_pp_34-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In 1818 the British decided not to become involved in continental issues that did not directly affect them. They rejected the plan of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia" title="Alexander I of Russia">Tsar Alexander I</a> to suppress future revolutions. The Concert system fell apart as the common goals of the Great Powers were replaced by growing political and economic rivalries.<sup id="cite_ref-C.W._Crawley_1830._pp_669-71_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-C.W._Crawley_1830._pp_669-71-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Artz says the Congress of Verona in 1822 "marked the end".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> There was no Congress called to restore the old system during the great <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">revolutionary upheavals of 1848</a> with their demands for revision of the Congress of Vienna\'s frontiers along national lines.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_policies">British policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: British policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>British foreign policy was set by <a href="/wiki/George_Canning" title="George Canning">George Canning</a> (1822\xe2\x80\x9327), who avoided close cooperation with other powers. Britain, with its unchallenged Royal Navy and increasing financial wealth and industrial strength, built its foreign policy on the principle that no state should be allowed to dominate the Continent. It wanted to support the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> as a bulwark against Russian expansionism. It opposed interventions designed to suppress democracy, and was especially worried that France and Spain planned to suppress the independence movement underway in Latin America. Canning cooperated with the United States to promulgate the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a> to persevere newly independent Latin American states. His goal was to prevent French dominance and allow British merchants access to the opening markets.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slave_trade">Slave trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Slave trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a></div>\n<p>An important liberal advance was the <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">abolition</a> of the international slave trade. It began with legislation in Britain and the United States in 1807, which was increasingly <a href="/wiki/Blockade_of_Africa" title="Blockade of Africa">enforced</a> over subsequent decades by the British Royal Navy under treaties Britain negotiated, or coerced, other nations into agreeing.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The result was a reduction of over 95% in the volume of the slave trade from Africa to the New World. About 1000 slaves a year were illegally brought into the United States, as well as some to Cuba and Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Slavery was <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_slavery_timeline" class="mw-redirect" title="Abolition of slavery timeline">abolished</a> in the British Empire in 1833, the <a href="/wiki/French_Second_Republic" title="French Second Republic">French Republic</a> <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_British_and_French_Caribbean" title="Slavery in the British and French Caribbean">in 1848</a>, the United States in 1865, and <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Brazil" title="Slavery in Brazil">Brazil in 1888</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain_loses_its_colonies">Spain loses its colonies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Spain loses its colonies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">Spanish American wars of independence</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bolivar_Arturo_Michelena.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bolivar_Arturo_Michelena.jpg/220px-Bolivar_Arturo_Michelena.jpg" width="220" height="440" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bolivar_Arturo_Michelena.jpg/330px-Bolivar_Arturo_Michelena.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Bolivar_Arturo_Michelena.jpg/440px-Bolivar_Arturo_Michelena.jpg 2x" data-file-width="874" data-file-height="1748" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bolivar_Arturo_Michelena.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Sim\xc3\xb3n Bol\xc3\xadvar, (1783\xe2\x80\x931830), a leader of independence in Latin America</div></div></div>\n<p>Spain was at war with Britain from 1798 to 1808, and the British Royal Navy cut off its contacts with its colonies. Trade was handled by neutral American and Dutch traders. The colonies set up temporary governments or juntas which were effectively independent from Spain. The division exploded between Spaniards who were born in Spain (called "peninsulares") versus those of Spanish descent born in <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a> (called "criollos" in Spanish or "creoles" in English) The two groups wrestled for power, with the criollos leading the call for independence and eventually winning that independence. Spain lost all of its American colonies, except Cuba and Puerto Rico, in a <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">complex series of revolts</a> from 1808 to 1826.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Multiple revolutions in Latin America allowed the region to break free of the mother country. Repeated attempts to regain control failed, as Spain had no help from European powers. Indeed, Britain and the United States worked against Spain, enforcing the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>.  British merchants and bankers took a dominant role in Latin America, In 1824, the armies of generals <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_San_Mart%C3%ADn" title="Jos\xc3\xa9 de San Mart\xc3\xadn">Jos\xc3\xa9 de San Mart\xc3\xadn</a> of Argentina and <a href="/wiki/Sim%C3%B3n_Bol%C3%ADvar" title="Sim\xc3\xb3n Bol\xc3\xadvar">Sim\xc3\xb3n Bol\xc3\xadvar</a> of Venezuela defeated the last Spanish forces; the final defeat came at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ayacucho" title="Battle of Ayacucho">Battle of Ayacucho</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/History_of_Peru" title="History of Peru">Peru</a>. After the loss of its colonies, Spain played a minor role in international affairs. Spain kept Cuba, which repeatedly revolted in three wars of independence, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_War_of_Independence" title="Cuban War of Independence">Cuban War of Independence</a>. The United States demanded reforms from Spain, which Spain refused. The U.S. <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War">intervened by war in 1898</a>. Winning easily, the U.S. took Cuba and gave it independence. The U.S. also took the Spanish colonies of the Philippines and Guam.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Though it still had small <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire#Territories_in_Africa_(1885\xe2\x80\x931975)" title="Spanish Empire">colonial holdings in North Africa</a>, Spain\'s role in international affairs was essentially over.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Greek_independence:_1821.E2.80.931833"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_independence:_1821\xe2\x80\x931833">Greek independence: 1821\xe2\x80\x931833</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Greek independence: 1821\xe2\x80\x931833">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Navarino" title="Battle of Navarino">Battle of Navarino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Navarino.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Navarino.jpg/300px-Navarino.jpg" width="300" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Navarino.jpg/450px-Navarino.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Navarino.jpg/600px-Navarino.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1990" data-file-height="1260" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Navarino.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Allied victory at Navarino (1827)</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a> was the major military conflict in the 1820s.  The Great powers supported the Greeks, but did not want the Ottoman Empire destroyed.  Greece was initially to be an autonomous state under Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a>, but by 1832, in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Constantinople_(1832)" title="Treaty of Constantinople (1832)">Treaty of Constantinople</a>, it was recognized as a fully independent kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After some initial success the Greek rebels were beset by internal disputes. The Ottomans, with major aid from Egypt, cruelly crushed the rebellion and harshly punished the Greeks.  Humanitarian concerns in Europe were outraged, as typified by English poet <a href="/wiki/Lord_Byron" title="Lord Byron">Lord Byron</a>.  The context of the three Great Powers\' intervention was Russia\'s long-running  expansion at the expense of the decaying Ottoman Empire. However Russia\'s ambitions in the region were seen as a major geostrategic threat by the other European powers. Austria feared the disintegration of the Ottoman Empire would destabilize its southern borders. Russia\'s gave strong emotional support for the fellow-<a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Christianity">Orthodox Christian</a> Greeks. The British were motivated by strong public support for the Greeks.  Fearing unilateral Russian action in support of the Greeks, Britain and France bound Russia by treaty to a joint intervention which aimed to secure Greek autonomy whilst preserving Ottoman territorial integrity as a check on Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Powers agreed, by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1827)" title="Treaty of London (1827)">Treaty of London (1827)</a>, to force the Ottoman government to grant the Greeks autonomy within the empire and despatched naval squadrons to Greece to enforce their policy.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The decisive Allied naval victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Navarino" title="Battle of Navarino">Battle of Navarino</a> broke the military power of the Ottomans and their Egyptian allies.  Victory saved the fledgling <a href="/wiki/First_Hellenic_Republic" title="First Hellenic Republic">Greek Republic</a> from collapse. But it required two more military interventions, by Russia in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_War_(1828%E2%80%931829)" title="Russo-Turkish War (1828\xe2\x80\x931829)">Russo-Turkish War of 1828\xe2\x80\x939</a> and by a <a href="/wiki/Morea_expedition" title="Morea expedition">French expeditionary force</a> to the Peloponnese to force the withdrawal of Ottoman forces from central and southern Greece and to finally secure Greek independence.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Travel.2C_trade_and_communications"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Travel,_trade_and_communications">Travel, trade and communications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Travel, trade and communications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width: -moz-min-content; width: -webkit-min-content; width: min-content; max-width: 100%;"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow: auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Lusitania_at_end_of_record_voyage_1907_LC-USZ62-64956.jpg" class="image" title="RMS&#160;Lusitania arriving in New York from Liverpool, England, in 1907. As the primary means of trans-oceanic voyages for over a century, ocean liners handled the travel needs of businessmen, immigrants and tourists."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_Lusitania_at_end_of_record_voyage_1907_LC-USZ62-64956.jpg/870px-The_Lusitania_at_end_of_record_voyage_1907_LC-USZ62-64956.jpg" width="870" height="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_Lusitania_at_end_of_record_voyage_1907_LC-USZ62-64956.jpg/1305px-The_Lusitania_at_end_of_record_voyage_1907_LC-USZ62-64956.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_Lusitania_at_end_of_record_voyage_1907_LC-USZ62-64956.jpg/1740px-The_Lusitania_at_end_of_record_voyage_1907_LC-USZ62-64956.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2637" data-file-height="667" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Lusitania_at_end_of_record_voyage_1907_LC-USZ62-64956.jpg" title="File:The Lusitania at end of record voyage 1907 LC-USZ62-64956.jpg"> </a></div><a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania">RMS&#160;<i>Lusitania</i></a> arriving in New York from Liverpool, England, in 1907. As the primary means of trans-oceanic voyages for over a century, ocean liners handled the travel needs of businessmen, immigrants and tourists.</div></div></div>\n<p>The world became much smaller as long-distance travel and communications improved dramatically. Every decade there were more ships, more scheduled destinations, faster trips, and lower fares for passengers and cheaper rates for merchandise. This facilitated international trade and international organization.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>  \n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Travel">Travel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Travel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hornetclippership.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hornetclippership.jpg/220px-Hornetclippership.jpg" width="220" height="361" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hornetclippership.jpg/330px-Hornetclippership.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hornetclippership.jpg/440px-Hornetclippership.jpg 2x" data-file-width="546" data-file-height="896" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hornetclippership.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Hornet_(clipper)" title="Hornet (clipper)">Hornet</a></i> \xe2\x80\x93 an American clipper ship of the 1850s</div></div></div>\n<p>Underwater telegraph cables linked the world\'s major trading nations by the 1860s.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cargo <a href="/wiki/Sailing_ship" title="Sailing ship">sailing ships</a> were slow; historians estimate that the average speed of all long-distance Mediterranean voyages to Palestine was only 2.8 knots.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>  Passenger ships achieved greater speed by sacrificing cargo space. The sailing ship records were held by the <a href="/wiki/Clipper" title="Clipper">clipper</a>, a very fast sailing ship of the 1843-69 era. Clippers were narrow for their length, could carry limited bulk freight, small by later 19th century standards, and had a large total sail area. Their average speed was six knots and they carried passengers across the globe, primarily on the trade routes between Britain and its colonies in the east, in trans-Atlantic trade, and the New York-to-San Francisco route round <a href="/wiki/Cape_Horn" title="Cape Horn">Cape Horn</a> during the <a href="/wiki/California_Gold_Rush" title="California Gold Rush">California Gold Rush</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The much faster steam-powered, iron-hulled <a href="/wiki/Ocean_liner" title="Ocean liner">ocean liner</a> became the dominant mode of passenger transportation from the 1850s to the 1950s.  It used coal\xe2\x80\x94and needed many coaling stations. After 1900 oil became the favoured fuel and did not require frequent refueling.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Freight rates on ocean traffic held steady in the 18th century down to about 1840, and then began a rapid downward plunge. The British dominated world exports and rates for British freight fell 70 percent, from 1840 to 1910.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>  The Suez Canal cut the shipping time from London to India by a third when it opened in 1869. The same ship could make more voyages in a year, so it could charge less and carry more goods every year.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Technological innovation was steady. Iron hulls replaced wood by mid-century; after 1870, steel replaced iron. It took much longer for steam engines to replace sails.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Note the sailing ship across from the <i>Lusitania</i> in the photograph above. Wind was free, and could move the ship at 2-3 knots<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This seems very slow for a sailing vessel (too slow to stem many tides), even a bulk cargo one - clippers (admittedly much faster) made 14+kts, see: https&#58;//www.theguardian.com/environment/2010/jul/25/slow-ships-cut-greenhouse-emissions (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, unless it was becalmed. Coal was expensive and required coaling stations along the route. A common solution was for a merchant ship to rely mostly on its sails, and only use the steam engine as a backup.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The first steam engines were very inefficient, using a great deal of coal. For an ocean voyage in the 1860s, half of the cargo space was given over to coal. The problem was especially acute for warships, because their combat range using coal was strictly limited. Only the British Empire had a network of coaling stations that permitted a global scope for the Royal Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> \nSteady improvement gave high-powered compound engines which were much more efficient. The boilers and pistons were built of steel, which could handle much higher pressures than iron. They were first used for high-priority cargo, such as mail and passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine engine</a> around 1907 dramatically improved efficiency, and the increasing use of oil after 1910 meant far less cargo space had to be devoted to the fuel supply.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communications">Communications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Communications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>By the 1850s, railways and telegraph lines connected all the major cities inside Western Europe, as well as those inside the United States. Instead of greatly reducing the need for travel, the telegraph made travel easier to plan and replaced the slow long-distance mail service.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Submarine_communications_cable" title="Submarine communications cable">Submarine cables</a> were laid to link the continents by telegraph, which was a reality by the 1860s.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="1830.E2.80.931850s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1830\xe2\x80\x931850s">1830\xe2\x80\x931850s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1830\xe2\x80\x931850s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a></div>\n<p>Britain continued as the most important power, followed by Russia, France, Prussia and Austria. The United States was growing rapidly in size, population and economic strength, especially after its defeat of Mexico in 1848. Otherwise it avoided international entanglements as the slavery issue became more and more divisive. The <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> was the most important war, especially because it disrupted the stability of the system. Britain strengthened its colonial system especially in India, while France rebuilt its empire in Asia and North Africa. Russia continued its expansion south (toward Persia) and east (into Siberia). The Ottoman Empire steadily weakened, losing control in parts of the Balkans to the new states of Greece and Serbia.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1839)" title="Treaty of London (1839)">Treaty of London</a>, signed in 1839, the Great Powers guaranteed the neutrality of Belgium. Germany called it a "scrap of paper" and violated it in 1914 by invasion, whereupon Britain declared war on Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_policies_2">British policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: British policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anti-Corn_Law_League" title="Anti-Corn Law League">Anti-Corn Law League</a></div>\n<p>The repeal in 1846 of the tariff on food imports, called the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a>, marked a major turning point that made  free trade the national policy of Great Britain into the 20th century. Repeal demonstrated the power of "Manchester-school" industrial interests over protectionist agricultural interests.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1830 to 1865, with a few interruptions, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Palmerston" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Palmerston">Lord Palmerston</a> set British foreign policy. His goal was to keep Britain dominant by maintaining the balance of power in Europe. He cooperated with France when necessary, but did not make permanent alliances with anyone. He tried to keep autocratic nations like Russia and Austria in check; he supported liberal regimes because they led to greater stability in the international system.  However he also supported the autocratic Ottoman Empire because it blocked Russian expansion.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgian_Revolution">Belgian Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Belgian Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Revolution" title="Belgian Revolution">Belgian Revolution</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wappers_-_Episodes_from_September_Days_1830_on_the_Place_de_l%E2%80%99H%C3%B4tel_de_Ville_in_Brussels.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Wappers_-_Episodes_from_September_Days_1830_on_the_Place_de_l%E2%80%99H%C3%B4tel_de_Ville_in_Brussels.JPG/300px-Wappers_-_Episodes_from_September_Days_1830_on_the_Place_de_l%E2%80%99H%C3%B4tel_de_Ville_in_Brussels.JPG" width="300" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Wappers_-_Episodes_from_September_Days_1830_on_the_Place_de_l%E2%80%99H%C3%B4tel_de_Ville_in_Brussels.JPG/450px-Wappers_-_Episodes_from_September_Days_1830_on_the_Place_de_l%E2%80%99H%C3%B4tel_de_Ville_in_Brussels.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Wappers_-_Episodes_from_September_Days_1830_on_the_Place_de_l%E2%80%99H%C3%B4tel_de_Ville_in_Brussels.JPG/600px-Wappers_-_Episodes_from_September_Days_1830_on_the_Place_de_l%E2%80%99H%C3%B4tel_de_Ville_in_Brussels.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3348" data-file-height="2250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wappers_-_Episodes_from_September_Days_1830_on_the_Place_de_l%E2%80%99H%C3%B4tel_de_Ville_in_Brussels.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Episode of the Belgian Revolution of 1830</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Egide_Charles_Gustave_Wappers" class="mw-redirect" title="Egide Charles Gustave Wappers">Gustaf Wappers</a> (1834)</div></div></div>\n<p>Catholic Belgians in 1830 broke away from the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_the_Netherlands" title="United Kingdom of the Netherlands">United Kingdom of the Netherlands</a> and established an independent <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Belgium" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Belgium">Kingdom of Belgium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> They could not accept the Dutch\'s king\'s favouritism toward Protestantism and his disdain for the French language. Outspoken liberals regarded <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_the_Netherlands" title="William I of the Netherlands">King William I\'s</a> rule as despotic. There were high levels of unemployment and industrial unrest among the working classes.  There was small-scale fighting but it took years before the Netherlands finally recognized defeat. In 1839 the Dutch accepted Belgian independence by signing the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1839)" title="Treaty of London (1839)">Treaty of London</a>. The major powers guaranteed Belgian independence.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Empire">Ottoman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ottoman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Foreign relations of the Ottoman Empire">Foreign relations of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>\n<p>The Ottoman Empire was only briefly involved in the Napoleonic Wars through the <a href="/wiki/French_campaign_in_Egypt_and_Syria" title="French campaign in Egypt and Syria">French campaign in Egypt and Syria</a>, 1798\xe2\x80\x931801. It was not invited to the Vienna Conference. During this period the Empire steadily weakened militarily, and lost most of its holdings in Europe (starting with Greece) and later in North Africa. Its great enemy was Russia, while its chief supporter was Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As the 19th century progressed the Ottoman Empire grew weaker and Britain increasingly became its chief ally and protector, even fighting the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> in the 1850s to help it out against Russia. Three British leaders played major roles. <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" title="Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Lord Palmerston</a> in the 1830-65 era considered the Ottoman Empire an essential component in the balance of power, was the most favourable toward Constantinople. <a href="/wiki/William_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="William Gladstone">William Gladstone</a> in the 1870s  sought to build a Concert of Europe that would support the survival of the empire. In the 1880s and 1890s <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a> contemplated an orderly dismemberment of it, in such a way as to reduce rivalry between the greater powers.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Serbian_independence">Serbian independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Serbian independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Revolution" title="Serbian Revolution">Serbian Revolution</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Serbia1817.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Serbia1817.png/250px-Serbia1817.png" width="250" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Serbia1817.png/375px-Serbia1817.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Serbia1817.png/500px-Serbia1817.png 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="623" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Serbia1817.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Serbia" title="Principality of Serbia">Principality of Serbia</a> in 1817</div></div></div>\n<p>A successful uprising against the Ottomans marked the foundation of <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Serbia" title="Principality of Serbia">modern Serbia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>  The Serbian Revolution took place between 1804 and 1835, as this territory evolved from an <a href="/wiki/Belgrade_Pashaluk" class="mw-redirect" title="Belgrade Pashaluk">Ottoman province</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Serbia" title="Constitution of Serbia">constitutional monarchy</a> and a modern <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>. The first part of the period, from 1804 to 1815, was marked by a violent struggle for independence with two armed uprisings. The later period (1815\xe2\x80\x931835) witnessed a peaceful consolidation of political power of the increasingly autonomous Serbia, culminating in the recognition of the right to hereditary rule by <a href="/wiki/List_of_Serbian_monarchs" title="List of Serbian monarchs">Serbian princes</a> in 1830 and 1833 and the territorial expansion of the young monarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The adoption of the first written <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Serbia" title="Constitution of Serbia">Constitution</a> in 1835 abolished <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and made the country <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerain</a><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crimean_War">Crimean War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Crimean War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a></div>\n<p>The <b>Crimean War</b> (1853\xe2\x80\x9356) was fought between Russia on the one hand and an alliance of Great Britain, France, Sardinia, and the Ottoman Empire on the other. Russia was defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1851 France under <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a> compelled the Sublime Porte (the Ottoman or Turkish government) to recognize it as the protector of Christian sites in the Holy Land. Russia denounced this claim, since it claimed to be the protector of all Eastern Orthodox Christians in the Ottoman Empire. France sent its fleet to the Black Sea; Russia responded with its own show of force. In 1851, Russia sent troops into the Ottoman provinces of Moldavia and Wallachia. Britain, now fearing for the security of the Ottoman Empire, sent a fleet to join with the French expecting the Russians would back down.\n</p><p>Diplomatic efforts failed. The Sultan declared war against Russia in October 1851. Following an Ottoman naval disaster in November, Britain and France declared war against Russia. Most of the battles took place in the Crimean peninsula, which the Allies finally seized. London, shocked to discover that France was secretly negotiating with Russia to form a postwar alliance to dominate Europe, dropped its plans to attack St. Petersburg and instead signed a one-sided armistice with Russia that achieved almost none of its war aims. \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edouard_Dubufe_Congr%C3%A8s_de_Paris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Edouard_Dubufe_Congr%C3%A8s_de_Paris.jpg/350px-Edouard_Dubufe_Congr%C3%A8s_de_Paris.jpg" width="350" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Edouard_Dubufe_Congr%C3%A8s_de_Paris.jpg/525px-Edouard_Dubufe_Congr%C3%A8s_de_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Edouard_Dubufe_Congr%C3%A8s_de_Paris.jpg/700px-Edouard_Dubufe_Congr%C3%A8s_de_Paris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1990" data-file-height="1210" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edouard_Dubufe_Congr%C3%A8s_de_Paris.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diplomats at the Congress of Paris, 1856, settled the Crimean War; painting by <a href="/wiki/Edouard_Louis_Dubufe" class="mw-redirect" title="Edouard Louis Dubufe">Edouard Louis Dubufe</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1856)" title="Treaty of Paris (1856)">Treaty of Paris</a> signed March 30, 1856, ended the war. It admitted the Ottoman Empire to the European concert, and the Powers promised to respect its independence and territorial integrity. Russia gave up a little land and relinquished its claim to a protectorate over the Christians in the Ottoman domains. The Black Sea was demilitarized, and an international commission was set up to guarantee freedom of commerce and navigation on the Danube River. Moldavia and Wallachia remained under nominal Ottoman rule, but would be granted independent constitutions and national assemblies.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>New rules of wartime commerce were set out: (1) privateering was illegal; (2) a neutral flag covered enemy goods except contraband; (3) neutral goods, except contraband, were not liable to capture under an enemy flag; (4) a blockade, to be legal, had to be effective.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The war helped modernize warfare by introducing major new technologies such as railways, the telegraph, and modern nursing methods. In the long run the war marked a turning point in Russian domestic and foreign policy. Russian intellectuals used the defeat to demand fundamental reform of the government and social system. The war weakened both Russia and Austria, so they could no longer promote stability. This opened the way for Napoleon III, <a href="/wiki/Camillo_Benso,_Count_of_Cavour" title="Camillo Benso, Count of Cavour">Cavour</a> (in Italy) and <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> (in Germany) to launch a series of wars in the 1860s that reshaped Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Moldavia_and_Wallachia">Moldavia and Wallachia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Moldavia and Wallachia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rom1793-1812.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rom1793-1812.png/250px-Rom1793-1812.png" width="250" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rom1793-1812.png/375px-Rom1793-1812.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rom1793-1812.png/500px-Rom1793-1812.png 2x" data-file-width="587" data-file-height="413" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rom1793-1812.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Moldavia, Wallachia and Transylvania in 1812. In 1859 Moldavia and Wallachia formed Romania, and acquired Transylvania in 1918.</div></div></div>\n<p>In a largely peaceful transition, the Ottoman provinces of <a href="/wiki/Moldavia" title="Moldavia">Moldavia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachia</a> broke away slowly, achieved effective autonomy by 1859, and finally became officially an independent nation in 1878. The two provinces had long been under Ottoman control, but both Russia and Austria also wanted them, making the region a site of conflict in the 19th century. The population was largely Orthodox in religion and spoke Romanian, but there were many minorities, such as Jews and Greeks. The provinces were occupied by Russia after the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Adrianople_(1829)" title="Treaty of Adrianople (1829)">Treaty of Adrianople</a> in 1829. Russian and Turkish troops combined to suppress the <a href="/wiki/Wallachian_Revolution_of_1848" title="Wallachian Revolution of 1848">Wallachian Revolution of 1848</a>. During the Crimean War Austria took control. The population decided on unification on the basis of historical, cultural and ethnic connections. It took effect in 1859 after the double election of <a href="/wiki/Alexandru_Ioan_Cuza" title="Alexandru Ioan Cuza">Alexandru Ioan Cuza</a> as Ruling Prince of the <a href="/wiki/United_Principalities" title="United Principalities">United Principalities of Moldavia and Wallachia</a> (renamed <i>of Romania</i> in 1862).<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>With Russian sponsorship, Romania officially became independent in 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> It focused its attention on <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">Transylvania</a>, a province of Hungary with about 2 million Romanians. Finally when the Austro-Hungarian Empire collapsed at the end of the First World War, Romania obtained Transylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="1860.E2.80.931871:_Nationalism_and_unification"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1860\xe2\x80\x931871:_Nationalism_and_unification">1860\xe2\x80\x931871: Nationalism and unification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 1860\xe2\x80\x931871: Nationalism and unification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The force of nationalism grew dramatically in the early and middle 19th century. To a large extent, and involved a cultural realization of cultural identity among the people sharing the same language and religious heritage. It was strong in the established countries, and was a powerful force for demanding more unity with or independence from Germans, Irish, Italians, Greeks, and the Slavic peoples of southeastern Europe. The strong sense of nationalism also grew in established independent nations, such as Britain and France.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Britain">Great Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Great Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston#Prime_Minister:_1859\xe2\x80\x931865" title="Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston \xc2\xa7&#160;Prime Minister: 1859\xe2\x80\x931865</a></div>\n<p>In 1859, following another short-lived Conservative government, Prime Minister Lord Palmerston and <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">Earl</a> Russell made up their differences, and Russell consented to serve as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> in a new Palmerston cabinet. It was the first true <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> Cabinet. This period was a particularly eventful one in the world outside Britain, seeing the <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Unification of Italy">Unification of Italy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Diplomacy of the American Civil War">Diplomacy of the American Civil War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> and the 1864 war over <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">Schleswig-Holstein</a> between Denmark and the German states.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>  Russell  and Palmerston kept Britain neutral in every case.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a> and <a href="/wiki/French%E2%80%93German_enmity" title="French\xe2\x80\x93German enmity">French\xe2\x80\x93German enmity</a></div>\n<p>Despite his promises in 1852 of a peaceful reign, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a>  could not resist the temptations of glory in foreign affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>  He was visionary, mysterious and secretive; he had a poor staff, and kept running afoul of his domestic supporters. In the end he was incompetent as a diplomat.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> After a brief threat of an invasion of Britain in 1851, France and Britain cooperated in the 1850s, with an alliance in the Crimean War, and a major trade treaty in 1860. However Britain viewed the Second Empire of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a> with increasing distrust, especially as the emperor built up his navy, expanded his empire and took up a more active foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Napoleon III did score some successes: he strengthened French control over Algeria, established bases in Africa, began the takeover of Indochina, and opened trade with China. He facilitated a French company building the Suez Canal, which Britain could not stop. In Europe, however, Napoleon failed again and again.  The Crimean war of 1854\xe2\x80\x931856 produced no gains.  War with Austria in 1859 facilitated the unification of Italy, and Napoleon was rewarded with the annexation of Savoy and Nice. The British grew annoyed at his intervention in Syria in 1860\xe2\x80\x9361. He angered Catholics alarmed at his poor treatment of the Pope, then reversed himself and angered the anticlerical liberals at home and his erstwhile Italian allies. He lowered the tariffs, which helped in the long run but in the short run angered owners of large estates and the textile and iron industrialists, while leading worried workers to organize. Matters grew worse in the 1860s as Napoleon nearly blundered into war with the United States in 1862, while his Mexican intervention in 1861\xe2\x80\x931867 was a total disaster.  Finally in the end he went to war with the Prussia 1870 when it was too late to stop the unification of all Germans, aside from Austria, under the leadership of Prussia. Napoleon had alienated everyone; after failing to obtain an alliance with Austria and Italy, France had no allies and was bitterly divided at home. It was disastrously defeated on the battlefield, losing Alsace and Lorraine. A.J.P. Taylor is blunt: "he ruined France as a great power."<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italian_unification">Italian unification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Italian unification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Italian_unification" title="Italian unification">Italian unification</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Italian-unification.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Italian-unification.gif/250px-Italian-unification.gif" width="250" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Italian-unification.gif/375px-Italian-unification.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Italian-unification.gif/500px-Italian-unification.gif 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Italian-unification.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The stages of Italian unification between 1829\xe2\x80\x9371</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>Risorgimento</i> was the era 1830\xe2\x80\x931870 that saw the emergence of a national consciousness. Italians achieved independence from Austria and from the Pope, securing national unification.
